VAT may have been a `simple tax' initially, but add on such factors as multiple rates, exemptions, reliefs, disallowances and more --  and you have a cornucopia of variables which can only compound and confuse the ordinary financial interactions of trade.  Recently published by LexisNexis, `VAT in the EU' is indispensable if you wish to gain a basic understanding of the differences in the administration, or interpretation of VAT across the EU, particularly as the rules differ from country to country.<br /><br />The book begins with a list of all twenty-seven EU members and their respective standard rates of VAT. Accountants, as well as lawyers advising on contractual terms and obligations, will find it indispensible. B00169N0BK|5.0|(Note: I'm using OS X Lion)<br /><br />Monitor Calibration: Even though the options are very limited (only 4 or 5 settings each for white point and luminance) the Colormunki does a very accurate job, better than the Datacolor Spyder 3 I have (which I would not recommend).<br /><br />Printer Calibration: It's done a great job of maxing out the potential of this inexpensive Lexmark C543 color laser printer that I have, which the manufacturer provides no standard ICC profiles for. I can't wait to get a real photo quality inkjet now. I also wish I had just bought this in the first place instead of the Spyder 3.<br /><br />The \\"2 target\\" printer calibration system and strip scanning capability of the instrument makes profiling printers very quick and easy, even with manual operation. (No click click click on 100+ color patches. Just run the thing over 5 strips, print another target, run it over 5 strips again.) The only drawback to this dual target system is that you won't be able to print a target on a printer at a computer lab somewhere then take it home to create the profile. (Though there may be some unsupported way around this, you'll still end up running back-and-forth as the content of the second target depends on what readings you get from the first one.) So to profile a printer you'll have to have the Colormunki connected to the same computer as the printer.<br /><br />To those having problems with printer profiling: You are *almost certainly* printing your targets without turning off ALL of the color management in the print driver and on the printer. I thought I knew how to do this on my system, and I still managed to mess it up the first time. If the first target doesn't print with at least some bright primary colored patches then you didn't manage to get all of the color management stuff turned off. Go through the settings with a fine-tooth comb and disable everything related to color correction. Also, you may want to reset both your printer and printer driver to default factory settings before going through everything to disable the color correction stuff. Resetting to defaults may save you a major amount of trouble.<br /><br />Once I got the printer driver settings figured out, profiling worked perfectly.<br /><br />Another thing, depending on the type of printer you have, the Colormunki profiling software may generate a CMYK profile instead of an RGB profile. Unfortunately, lots of software (including print drivers) doesn't work with CMYK profiles (for example, Lightroom is only compatible with RGB profiles). If you have an ink jet printer then it's probably using an RGB data stream and you'll end up with an RGB profile, but other things like laser printers will give you a CMYK profile that you may only be able to use with something like Photoshop (which will convert RGB color spaces into CMYK.) This tripped me up for a while until I figured out what was going on. For now I'm stuck with having to print everything through Photoshop (or some other CMYK producing software) to use my profile to print my photos to the laser printer, but the results are good enough that I don't mind too much.<br /><br />Spot Measurements: The munki also comes with Photo ColorPicker for measuring any random opaque objects you want to know the exact color of. The only real problem that I see with this is that the device can have trouble collecting spot measurements from some non-flat surfaces. For example, I tried to read the patches off of my ColorChecker Passport and the thing isn't flat enough (due to the plastic case) to get the measurement port solidly against each patch. This doesn't affect monitor and printer profiling in the least, but it could be a little annoying for those trying to measure the color of random objects. To get something without this issue, though, you'll be spending a lot more on a spectrophotometer. In any case, the software it comes with for spot measurements is pretty good, and it does work on most things.<br /><br />Bundled Software Quality: The included software runs smoothly and does what you need for multiple monitor and printer calibration, I just wish it included some utilities for emissive spot measurement and creating basic Spectral Power Distribution plots. (Photo ColorPicker is great for \\"collecting\\" spot colors from objects, but it only supports reflective measurements and doesn't do SPDs.) All of the software that comes in the box is pretty simple and doesn't offer a lot of fancy advanced features. The most elaborate software in the box is probably the ColorPicker.<br /><br />Other Software: NEC's SpectraView II (for calibrating their displays) recognizes the ColorMunki and it does a pretty good job on their own MultiSync PA241 even compared to their own custom colorimeter puck. Currently I'm trying to evaluate whether or not it's even worth buying NECs puck if you already have a ColorMunki, but I haven't come to any conclusions yet. (Their puck is more accurate at really low luminance levels like 10cd/cm3, but that's about it.)<br /><br />Even without the SPD plot and emissive spot measurement features in the bundled software, I was still able to get SPD plots and emissive spot measurements (among other things) by downloading Argyll CMS (free). The only issue with Argyll CMS is that it's lacking a slick GUI (you'll have to open a terminal and use command line tools like 'spotread -S' to get SPD plots, even on Mac), but it seems to work perfectly with the Colormunki. It's also free, whereas similar CMS software with a nice GUI will cost you $100-$500 or more.<br /><br />Other Goodies: It looks like they're now shipping the Colormunki with a small ColorChecker Classic calibration card (for creating DNG camera calibration profiles) making it an even better deal. I had previously bought a [[ASIN:B002NU5UW8 X-Rite ColorChecker Passport]] for around $100 which is almost (but not quite) the same as what's now included for \\"free\\" with the Colormunki.<br /><br />Overall, I would recommend this to anyone needing an affordable solution for display and printer calibration. If you think you'll do any color printing at all, ever, or ever need to measure spot colors, then just skip the display-only colorimeters and get this thing! Otherwise you may end up buying this eventually anyway, and selling off your colorimeter.t you need for multiple monitor and printer calibration, I just wish it included some utilities for emissive spot measurement and creating basic Spectral Power Distribution plots. 1400069181|5.0|Warning: Don't read this review or this book unless you are ready to have some kind of change in your life.     About a week and a half ago I received two copies of the book \\"Start Something That Matters\\" by Blake Mycoskie. I was of course excited to read this book because of the TOMS story, but what I didn't expect was the book to have such an impact on my heart.  The opening of the book sets the tone off just right. Starting with the TOMS story and how Blake began his mission to give shoes to children helps shape the book into the inspirational theme it holds within the rest of the 165 pages. Blake writes the TOMS story with his heart on his sleeve, giving the details of his journey and even inserting a few journal pages and other visuals from the start of the the company. His humor shines throughout the first chapter but is mixed with sincere moments of motivation. \\"If you're like me and most people I know, you yearn for something more than jsut business success. You're searching for meaning. You want to have the time and freedom to do the things you love and contribute to making the world a better place,\\" write Blake, and it's true. This isn't a book for people just simply looking to start a new business, but for people looking to change the world at all costs, hence the title :\\"Start Something That Matters\\".  As teh book progresses the heart is impacted more and more but not just a simple movement to emotions happens. No, as Blake intertwines both his story, the beginning of TOMS and some stories of people who also started something that mattered to them and the world in some shape or form, the reader's heart feels as if it is starting to be changed. As I read page after page about a company I love mixed in with companies I see but never think about (and one I work for!) my heart wanted to join in their forces to change the world and Blake gives steps to do so.  From finding your story, to how to build trust in your company or movement, to having limited resources and staying frugal throughout, Blake builds this motivation in the spirit of whoever reads this book. I wish I could just reiterate the entire book for you! It's jsut so full of tips and websites and stories to check out and use that it is a must read for everyone who is wanting to do something big in the world.  But this book could be useful for even those who are already married to their favorite job but are hoping to make some little changes to it. Blake writes on how to treat your employees with helpful tips like getting rid of titles, treating your staff to fun things, giving time for the staff to give back and even making them feel like they can trust the people that work with them.  The book closes out with another story. This time a story that came to life because of a young man who found himself inspired by the TOMS story (which wraps the book up full circle). It's the final tug on the heart of the reader, the final call to motion in the reader's life and the beginning smile of a new venture for the person who is willing to take Blake's message and run with it.  Blake leaves the reader with a final life lesson: putting on your shoes to go. \\"Someone once told me the key to staying healthy was tying his shoes. What he meant was, one he actually put his shoes on and tied them, then he would go out for a run. And by going for a run, he was able to stay very healthy. The same philosophy applies here.\\"  Not being an avid reader of inspirational or even business books, I found this book helpful in many ways. I wanted to read it because of the TOMS story but found myself glued to every page, and if it wasn't for work I would have finished it in one sitting. It's definetly worth a second, third or even fourth read, but most of all its worth taking on Blake's challenges and finding not only your passion, but your story and then Starting Something That Matters. As Blake signs out his letters \\"Carpe Diem\\", seizing the day is exactly what you will be trying to do when you close this short, but impactful book that should never clutter your bookshelf but be work out sitting on the coffee table, travelling in your bag or being carried by hand. It's a guide worth keeping around at all times. A must- read.

B005PTQPU0|1.0|It's a measure of Kate Bush's lack of self awareness that she should have chosen the sort of album title (yes, yes, the Eskimos - whoops, sorry \\"Inuits\\" - have 50 words for snow) those lampooning her may have chosen for her tendency towards tortuous pseudo-intellectualism, although the fact alternative luvvie Stephen Fry co-sings on the title track would leave anyone in little doubt about that anyway. Somehow it doesn't come as a surprise to learn she was the partial inspiration for the Harry Potter character Hermione Grainger, right down to the burst couch hairdo.  Solo artists and those whose \\"bands\\" are little more than backing bands are always in danger of the artiste disappearing up their own wotsit, but Kate Bush is of course the famous moral tale of where it gets you. Just a pity she never learned the lesson.  Kate Bush was a success purely and simply because her over the top stage & TV performances that would have relegated her to a cult artist with one novelty hit in \\"Wuthering Heights\\" instead were perfect for the New Wave/Punk zeitgeist of the late 1970s/early 1980s. Like Ian Dury, she was the right sort of British oddball at the right time.  After a series of hit albums and riding the wave of pop culture feminism that saw her as high-brow (though as her competing sisterhood at that point consisted of the likes of the Nolan Sisters, it was hardly an epic battle of wits) and adopted by the whole alternative counter culture of the late 1970s/early 1980s right down to doing the coveted Amnesty International \\"Secret Policeman's Ball Slot\\" (although Not The Nine O'Clock News did lampoon her intellectual self-delusions with the \\"Them Heavy People\\" parody \\"England My Leotard\\"), Kate discovered her paying public had little interest in being culturally 'educated' by her as \\"The Dreaming\\" and its singles were monumental flops.  Taking a leaf from CBS's book in dealing with The Clash, EMI told her bluntly they'd not release another record until they could be sure itwould be a hit: \\"Running Up That Hill\\", \\"Cloudbursting\\" and the album \\"Hounds Of Love\\" later, all seemed to be back on track - until \\"The Sensual World\\" proved she'd only done enough to keep her in contract before reverting back to unintented self-parody of her own ever-growing pretentiousness.  Meanwhile a certain Bjork waiting in the wings took on the mantle of music's eccentric genius, and Bush joined Cliff Richard and Paul McCartney in being one of those EMI hasbeens kept on more for the sake of back catalogue sales than any hope of any new material seizing the public's enthusiasm.  Not that she needs to worry much about alienating anyone: Bush's audience by then, as now, had been relegated to those ageing inverted snobs that turn their noses up at mainstream pop and classical music alike, sneering at the former's commercialism and - ironically - the latter's elitism. Show me today's Kate Bush fan, and I'll show you a Guardian reading weekend eco-warrior who never quite outgrew their undergraduate pretending-to-be-\\"into\\"-something-to-appear-cultured phase that boasts they have a black belt in Feng Shui.  So to \\"50 Words Of Snow\\", on the back of this year's flogging-a-dead-horse \\"remake\\" album (ever popular with aged musicians out of ideas wanting to use their remaining diehard but gullible fans as milch cows) that's already drawn predictable sycophantic praise from those that would hail as \\"genius\\" an album of Kate Bush farting through a kazoo.  Sure, if your familiarity with piano driven music doesn't go beyond Bruce Hornsby And The Range you might think \\"50 Words For Snow\\" as being the Cream Twinkie of music, anyone else will note that every song is as good as the last because largely every song is the same as the last, tedious in its studied ponderousness or copying everything Peter Gabriel has been doing badly over the last decade.  Likely to be sucking your will to live over the speakers of every Cappuchino bar within a 500 mile radius for the next few months. Even the \\"completists\\" are best advised to wait for it to appear in the bargain buckets before buying.

0393064476|4.0|One point not noted in reviews so far? I find the New Historicism taught by Greenblatt to infuse this popular work as it has this professor's scholarly studies. As with Lucretius, about whom we still know practically nothing more than what Poggio did, so for Shakespeare, perhaps, about whom what we know has been debated for centuries?<br /><br />Matching the approach in Greenblatt's first work addressed beyond the seminar table, \\"Will in the World,\\" \\"The Swerve\\" works best when it elaborates the context as well as explicates the texts by the historically elusive author examined. To paraphrase, say, atoms and void-- nothing else (75) is a great way to sum up Lucretius' visionary, secularized impact, in the period (as Flaubert's quoted) between the time of gods and God, so to speak, when the pagans had a breather to judge their cosmos and civitas by their own estimations and measurements rather than those of Jove or Jesus. Now, this marked slant against religion rankles many fellow reviewers, as this anti-clerical tone enlivens the book, but consider how Greenblatt incorporates the barbed, righteous criticism of domestic secretary to the first John XXIII himself in this. Poggio's jokebook was popular in the Curia, but it was outnumbered by his moral treatises, castigating the corruption of his Church.<br /><br />Poggio's went on duty with his Roman master ca. 1415 to the Council of Constance, saw his pope deposed, and presumed to have witnessed Jerome of Prague burned alive after an eloquent Latin defense of his support of the similarly doomed Jan Hus. In New Historicist-popular fashion, Greenblatt figures these series of confrontations might have spurred the now-unemployed Poggio to have gone off to Fulda to find the Lucretian manuscript, as solace and to fuel his book mania. I find this convincing, even if as elsewhere in this work this scenario must remain speculative. The book tends to roam about, with necessarily \\"might have\\" and \\"could\\" as in \\"Will in the World.\\" Greenblatt skillfully makes his case, even if this rambles at times too widely, but it moves gracefully and at times very movingly. Any exposure of this Renaissance milieu and its classical foundation surely is welcome when few books aimed at a wider audience by professors address serious issues with verve and insight.<br /><br />Greenblatt with Poggio as with Shakespeare connects what we do know (and a lot of background detail, vivid examples, and tangential tales about flagellating monks, volcanically charred papyri, and bookworms) with what we cannot prove but can reasonably suppose. I liked this book, and adjusting its angle to account for the New Historicist perspective may help those readers who wonder why this professor constructs it as he does. May it also spark a renewed appreciation of Lucretius himself.fully makes his case, even if this rambles at times too widely, but it moves gracefully and at times very movingly. B000HEWEDK|5.0|So much has been written about this film, I'm sure I won't add anything you may not already have read.<br /><br />The intelligence with which this film was written is an argument that thinking people want to be challenged by what they are being offered. The medium is not the message, but the message can be conveyed by the medium.<br /><br />The power of ideas reigns supreme in good writing. The visual aspects of a film have to contribute, not overwhelm, to the overall story, the ideas, being presented for our consideration. This film balances both very well.<br /><br />And for a film made in 1956, viewing it today is every bit as powerful as the ideas conveyed in another classic, \\"Metropolis\\", from 1926. While there maybe elements that 'date' such films, the power of ideas clearly keeps these kinds of films relevant to today. Why? Because the ideas are timeless, the topics are the stuff of the human condition, which never changes, no matter the era in which they are portrayed.<br /><br />And when a film addresses these deeper kinds of things, when done with intelligence and with care for the way it is presented, absent \\"flavour of the moment\\" distractions, such films are destined to become classics that withstand the test of time - because the topics themselves are timeless.

B001DD0HPG|4.0|In this age of so many cookie-cutter guitarists who never miss a note and sound indistinguishable from one another, its good to be reminded of what a revelation Julian Bream was. Here was a player every bit as individual as Andrs Segovia, but more disciplined and expressively sophisticated. Further, Bream was far more catholic in his taste, embracing both thorny modern fare and early music. Following Breams recording career as he explored his varied interests was a delightful adventure. (Perhaps no other musician of the time showed just how great the renaissance lute repertoire really is.) One wonders how many of todays young guitarists, carefully groomed to never offend pedestrian tastes, will ever display similar artistic integrity.  This ten CD set is billed as a reissue of ten original LP recordings released between 1960 and 1983. As it turns out, the billing isnt entirely true. One of the releases was a 1979 two LP set of live performances by Bream and Williams during their 1978 three city tour of the United States. In this new set, three performances that were on the original release are omitted: Faures Dolly Suite, Sors Fantasie, Op. 54, and the Brahms Theme and Variations, Op. 18. Why Sony chose to omit these is a mystery, as all three works easily could have been included on the CDwith these works missing, it times out to a paltry 37:30. Whats particularly galling is that the original LP jacket is reproduced for each CD sleeve. Thus, buyers of this new compilation can see for themselves that theyve been cheated out of the complete 1979 release, as the three missing works are listed right on the original jacket artwork. Whats the thinking that goes into such an obvious insult to the buyer?  This is an unfortunate pattern that Sony has long followed with its reissues of Bream recordings, chopping up his original discography piecemeal. To younger buyers, its perhaps not a problem. But to those of us who grew up on and treasure these old Bream recordings, its infuriating.  One can quibble with the selections for these ten recordings. Breams playing began to slip during the late 1970s. So I tend to favor his earlier recordings. For my taste, Id like to have seen these included:  Music for Voice and GuitarJulian Bream & Peter Pears (1965) Lute Music From The Royal Courts Of Europe (1966) Julian BreamClassic Guitar (1969) Julian Bream Plays Villa-Lobos, with Andr Previn and the London Symphony (1971)  And no Bream collection is complete without his astonishing Popular Classics for Spanish Guitar (1963), although Sony has mercifully reissued this uncut. But this Original Jacket Collection does include three of my favorites: The Art of Julian Bream (1960), The Woods So Wild (1972), and also Breams seminal 20th Century Guitar (1967).  To be sure, Breams recorded output was uneven. Sample the out-of-tune playing on his Romantic Guitar (1970) release. And his tone could be a bit grating, something that got worse as time went on. Further, RCA and Bream often favored a recorded sound that exacerbated rather than softened his faults. Oddly, its the earliest recording in this set, The Art of Julian Bream, that I find most flattering to Breams playing. But whatever his faults, Bream was always a compelling player.  Throughout this review, I speak of Bream in the past tense only because he has retired as a concert artist. Bream is still very much with usindeed, hes actively commissioning and sponsoring performances of new works for the guitar. Even in retirement, Bream is a force for expanding the guitar repertoire.  So deduct one star for Sonys callous omission. And heres a bit of advice to Sony: the next time you reissue an original recording, include the entire recording, not bits of it.  -----------------------------------------------------------------------  Update: Sony re-released the complete Bream RCA recordings as a box set in 2013. The missing worksnoted above are included in the complete set.

B001FB5634|1.0|Yes, Yes, Yes. The Road is well acted, cinematically lush even in its sparceness, and emotionally engaging. For me, the most important question after seeing it was, \\"How can such a riveting film be so blind to so much?\\" The only answer I can come up with is that the book's author was blind, the screen writer was blind, the director and actors were blind, and everyone raving about the film is blind to its many serious flaws in content and imagination.  I preface the following thoughts by saying that, as a teenager, I lived through an event of some days and, to a lesser extent, some weeks similar to the devastation that provides the backdrop and apparent rationale for how everyone behaved in this film. And, people did not treat themselves and one another ANYTHING like they did in this film. Granted, my ordeal did not grind on for months and years until starvation and fatigue stripped all humanity from the minds and hearts of those who saw too much and remained alive too long. But the 1964 \\"Good Friday\\" earthquake that isolated communities all across Alaska, sometimes for weeks, did plunge many into a relatively deep and abiding state of privation.  So, let's first wonder what the overall point of a book and film might be that sets itself so far down a bleak chain of events that human beings are over the edge of extintion? Does anybody doubt that prolonged, extreme privation can strip us of every virtue, and reduce us to udder terror, dispair, and the inhuman treatment of others? Do we benefit in any way in our daily lives by being given such things to watch, by watch them, by feel ourselves entertained, and by praising those who made them?  If we try to decode and tease out the meaning of this film, what do we find? Do we find that demoralization in adversity is stronger than a mother's love and protective instincts for her child and husband? Do we find that a father will harm and kill others out of vindictiveness and unreasonable fear for the life of his own son? Dowe find that men will band together for the sake of torturing, killing, and eating others? And this we find hypnotically entertaining?  My observation is that we are more and better than these things. In dire circumstances, I have seen a mother give her life trying to protect her children from the cold, but I have never seen one give up and commit suicide. I have seen a man harm another to protect his family but only when all hope of escape was gone. I have seen men rise to a dire occasion, arm themselves to the teeth, and become the law in the jungle, but I have never seen them do anything but help the weak and the injured.  We are a species different from others. Yes, we are addicted to the objects of our senses, but we have empathy, compassion, and creative intelligence enough to act above our addictions. When things are at their worst, we ARE at our best. I submit that the author of the book, the screenwriter, the director, and the actors in the movie have NEVER been in the situation they have created for our entertainment. I submit that, by virtue of this fact, they have no basis other than idle and ignorant conjecture for showing us a fictional depiction of their own fear and ignorance. I submit that there is no socially redeeming quality or element to this film. It teaches us nothing positive and it only sews unreasonable fear and demoralization.   When are we going to turn our backs on such things, and use the time to do what we can to bring more Good into the world?

She's given this buddy-buddy story a lightness of touch that feels real and isn't forced. In fact, the constant wit and pranks reflect just how guys are when they're in real emotional turmoil - downright childish even. Kudos should also go to the four male leads who had to gel together convincingly, muck in on the arduous trek and physically brave the coastal weather - none of which can have been easy - or in the case of the freezing Irish sea - deeply unpleasant. And when the reality of their friend's terminal illness slowly begins to kick in and intrude on their 'fun' - they're skills as good actors make you 'feel' the heaviness of what their characters are all trying to block out.<br /><br />Principal lead Benedict Cumberbatch has many fans - and on the strength of his acting chops both here and on \\"Sherlock\\" (the UK TV series) - it's easy to see why. His James is a demanding lead-role - a frustrated 29-year old who is being eaten-alive by cancer. His longing to live - to have more time - to realise at least some of his dreams - are always at the surface - and you ache for him as he slips in and out of a morphine-induced stupor that is numbing his escalating physical agony. As well as the pain - James has to also contend with his squabbling, tetchy pals - who are trying to be cool about it all but are freaked out inside and increasingly awkward with his now impending loss.<br /><br />His fellow travellers consist of Adam Robertson as the witty and yet dependable Bill, Tom Burke as the needy but sincere Davy and JJ Feild as the handsome devil-may-care Miles (surely a new Jude Law in the making). They will even encounter Hugh Bonneville (of Downton Abbey fame) as a nutty Beachcomber looking for rare Star Wars toys. The dialogue is playful, smart and confrontational when it's needed. And then they're faced with the ultimate true-friendship decision - but can any or all of them - actually go through with it? Even with this much sadness at its core and end, \\"Third Star\\" remains hopeful - a sign of a great film rather than just a good one.<br /><br />EXTRAS include a brief 'Making Of' and two shorts by Hattie Dalton called \\"The Banker\\" and \\"One Of Those Days\\". The only real let down for me is that it's on DVD only - and not BLU RAY - where all that great camera work would surely shine even more.<br /><br />There are moments in \\"Third Star\\" when you genuinely ache for James' loss - when you hurt at the arbitrary cruelty of life - snubbing out one person, but letting another live.  This is a very good movie - one that celebrates life and makes you think about it too.<br /><br />Put it high on your rental/to buy list...29-year old who is being eaten-alive by cancer. 0307593312|4.0|Part romance, part thriller, 1Q84 explores what can happen to people who live in a world without love.  In the spring of 1984, Aomame,  a health club trainer , jumps out of a cab stalled in traffic on an elevated expressway and descends a ladder into a strange world she labels 1Q84, a place where day to day reality has gone off kilter.  We quickly learn that Aomame has a grisly sideline: she uses her skills as a physiotherapist to dispatch men who brutalize women.  Raised by religious fanatics, Aomame left home early and has grown into a tough, competent, emotionally contained adult.  The high point of her romantic life happened at age ten, when she reached out to grasp the hand of Tengo, a boy in her class.  She hasn't seen him since.<br /><br />Tengo had a rough childhood himself as the only son of an emotionally distant fee collector for NHK Television.  Now in his late twenties, Tengo teaches math at a cram school and is attempting to make it as a fiction writer.  An editor friend convinces Tengo to rewrite a fantasy novel by a female teenager who has submitted her story about little people who emerge from the mouth of a dead goat and make chrysalises out of air.  From these chrysalises hatch replicas of real people.  The girl author, Fuka-Eri, has run away from Sakigake, a religious cult led by her father.  Once the book is published, Tengo gradually discovers is that Fuka-Eri's fantastic story may in fact be true.  The novel's point of view alternates between Aomame and Tengo as they make halting progress toward reconnecting.  Tengo and Aomame's path back is blocked by the Sakigake cult, which feels threatened by Fuka-Eri's and Tengo's novelistic collaboration.<br /><br />Although the book is about 100 pages too long, (especially the sections dealing with a detective hired by the cult to track down Aomame), it's compulsively readable.  Many of Murakami's signature preoccupations are here.  The characters glide between a quotidian world of cooking, cleaning, going to the bar and a dreamlike world of two moons, immaculate conceptions, mind reading cult leaders and little troll people. Many of his books have Japanese teenage girls as characters.  The one here, Fuka-Eri, is part sprite, part nymphet, a character who fits perfectly into the alternate reality of 1Q84.  Murakami doesn't shy away from her sexual power, indeed sex in many variations saturates this novel, sex for the most part divorced from love.  Murakami has explored the world of cults before in Underground, his superb non-fiction examination of the Aum Shinrikyo cult that released poison sarin gas on the Tokyo subways.  The Sakigake cult in 1Q84 has its own closed circuit logic that threatens the safety and happiness of the book's main characters.<br /><br />Murkami's consistent message is that the reality created by human minds is an unstable construct.  The best parts of 1Q84 depict the struggles of people with blunted emotions trying to make their way back to the \\"real\\" world.  Although Murakami leaves several plot threads dangling, this is a powerful, richly imagined and emotionally satisfying look at how men and women who can't connect with others wind up existing in a pale approximation of reality.nd a dreamlike world of two moons, immaculate conceptions, mind reading cult leaders and little troll people. 1451627289|5.0|I'm what you'd call one of the \\"prodigal\\" Stephen King fans: I loved his work, then for a while I didn't, then within the last eight years or so he's turned me around again. There are other types of fans, but I find that my style of fandom is the second most common, the first being people who used to like him, then stopped, then didn't pick him up again, so really they're \\"former fans\\". His most recent collection is one of those books that if you're a former fan it will make you an prodigal fan.<br /><br />The premise is dirt-simple: a school teacher is shown a portal that takes a person back in time to one day in 1958 and, after some experimentation and some cajoling by the portal's owner, he is convinced to go back in time to try and stop the assassination of JFK. It's not horror. It's technically a science fiction novel with large swathes of dramatic story...LARGE swathes.<br /><br />If you think horror is King's crutch, you will be pleasantly surprised. All of the tools that King has made his bread and butter with - resilient first person narrative, everyday folks thrust in spectacular situations, curve balls - are in full display here, but without the overwhelming supernatural element. The horrors here come from regular folks, from man's struggle against himself.<br /><br />It's a captivating read, paced well (though some of the third part is a little long in tooth, but by then you're in it for the long haul) and he resolves it splendidly, circumventing his long-held Achilles' heel of weak and abrupt endings. He must have been taking finishing classes from Frank Darabont, who has been cleaning up the endings in his films since \\"Shawshank\\".<br /><br />If you're a fan, you'll dig it. If you're a former fan, you'll dig it. If you've never read King before because you don't like horror, this is a great place to absolve yourself of the misguided notion that horror is all he writes, or writes well. A great book in the JFK fiction canon, and a pearl of a story in King's own track record.

0571283578|3.0|I have read many sequels to Jane Austen novels - most of them dreadful - and was very much looking forward to this one because it is by an author I respect and enjoy. I do however suspect that she had more fun writing this book than I did reading it. While it was neatly and professionally done, as you might expect from an author of this calibre, it lacked much of the charm, humour and lightness of touch of the original. Indeed, in stating categorically that Elizabeth Bennett was not imaginative, I was left wondering if the author had read the same original novel that I had.  The language occasionally grated. Jane Austen's language was immensely complicated at times but all to good purpose and never unnecessarily so. Too many writers of fan fiction seem to feel they must emulate the complexity simply for the sake of it and that was a little the case here at times. Also, freely using contractions such as 'I'm and it's' for the dialogue so constantly, grated on me. It was too modern and an editor should have been onto that very sharply.  There was far, far too much retelling of the original story. If the reader has not read the original, they are highly unlikely to be buying this sequel. Whole chapters devoted to slavish retelling of the original plot, almost in the same words and with lots of quotes, are unnecessary and turgid. I also understand the clever thinking behind using some of the characters from other Jane Austen books as a crossover, but it simply doesn't work in this book. It has the effect of making those sections slightly cartoonish.  We needed far more of Jane and Bingley. They are not exciting characters in their own right but it would have been interesting to know what had happened to them in the intervening years and for them to have gained some colour during that time. B004G8QTS4|5.0|Boone Daniels, the ex-SDPD surfing PI returning from Don Winslow's spellbinding \\"The Dawn Patrol,\\" is in a bit of a funk.  The Pacific's August swell is about as wild as a frog pond, Boone's leggy blonde lover Sunny D has gone pro and is surfing somewhere in the south Pacific, but worst of all, seems that some are abandoning the unwritten surfer code of \\"peace, friendship, tolerance, and individuality\\" and are (gasp) forming gangs and protecting their patches of the ocean.  With that, the cagey Winslow, for my money the world's foremost expert on the combination of surf culture, SoCal, and drug trafficking, shreds another installment of crime, Zen, and Surfbonics wrapped around a new gig for Boone that threatens a dawn patrol wipeout.<br /><br />So \\"The Gentleman's Hour\\" is not only the novel that follows \\"The Dawn Patrol,\\" but also the hours that follow it as well - that time of the morning when Pacific Beach's veteran and legendary surfers roll out of bed and head for the sand - those gray-hairs who, without the inconvenience of regular jobs, can hit the surf at a more civilized hour.  It is in this transition that Boone runs into Dan Nichols, a San Diego success story who took his small inherited fortune and turned it into a very large fortune on the threads of an internationally distributed line of his own surf clothing.  But Dan, unaffected, still cool and still surfing, has a problem: he thinks his trophy wife is cheating on him, and he wants Boone to close the case.  Boone hates the sleaze and voyeurism of adultery cases, but Nichols has the proverbial offer-he-can't-refuse, and before the next wave break Boone is off to the local spy store to stock up on surveillance gear.<br /><br />But Dan Nichols and fidelity problems are merely subterfuge in this fast moving, multi-threaded crime mystery crafted for the ADD set: the real deal is the murder of Kelly Kuhio, a Pacific Beach legend and nearly father figure to Boone, a frequenter of the gentleman's hour who enjoys deity status in and around San Diego's bars and beaches.  Kuhio is murdered in a senseless crime to which Corey Blasingame, the spoiled loser son of a well-healed La Jolla real estate developer, confesses, instantly claiming the title of most despised man in southern California.  Until, that is, Boone agrees to join \\"The Dawn Patrol's\\" hottie Brit lawyer Petra \\"Pete\\" Hall as the defense team's investigator, distancing Boone from his laid back board-toting posse.  Neat crime investigation and well-drawn characters add depth and credibility; local flavor closes the deal.  Add to the wash the shadowy and sadistic \\"Mr. Jones\\" - a one-man torture chamber for a brutal Mexican drug cartel - neo-Nazi martial arts punks, cynical humor, and a generous dose of sexual tension between Boone and Pete for a eclectic mix of frenzy and chill that twists and turns along the way, managing to neatly mesh for a satisfying - and surprising - conclusion.<br /><br />Winslow is the unsung bard of the Pacific Coast Highway; hip, and irreverent, a master storyteller who knows the life of boards and babes and drugs and thugs as he chronicles with authority and panache California's wealth, greed, shallowness and natural beauty.  From serious epics like \\"The Power of the Dog\\" to chilled beach fare like this one, Don Winslow is a talented author who demands to be read - and definitely deserves space on the silver screen. B005J29HCQ|5.0|British novelist Zadie Smith published a collection of occasional essays entitled Changing My Mind (Penguin 2009). Brian Wilson's 2004 reconstruction of the Beach Boys' legendary Smile album was a palpable disappointment to me. However, after listening to the Beach Boys' two-CD set culled from those sessions, I've concluded it was well worth the 44-year wait.  The Beach Boys were Brian Wilson's instrument much as the orchestra was Duke Ellington's. Vocally, these sessions outstrip Wilson's version by miles. They could sing like angels even when the group was in serious trouble. The arrangements are superior to either Wilson's version or the Boys' re-recordings of tracks like \\"Surf's Up\\" and \\"Wind Chimes\\" on Smiley Smile, the uneven record that followed in the wake of the aborted sessions. Though even on this rendition the crunching bombast of its rhythmic patterns can be numbing.  But Wilson was still actively engaged in the production, and was intimate with the Los Angeles studio musicians, many of whom were members of Phil Spector's \\"wrecking crew.\\" You can hear their lively interplay on the second disk. While the set doesn't indicate who the musicians are, I suspect that many of them were on Pet Sounds too.  What I hear now is the whimsy, modesty, and outright silliness of \\"Do You Like Worms (Roll Plymouth Rock),\\" \\"Cabin Essence,\\" \\"Vega-Tables,\\" \\"Barnyard,\\" and \\"Wind Chimes.\\" In their sonic experimentation the sessions are Beatlesque, yes, but they also point to the quotidian dailiness of McCartney's solo albums, even in the musique concrete of construction noise--hammering, sawing, a thumb-smashed \\"Oww.\\"  Except for \\"God Only Knows,\\" I'm not fond of religiosity in pop music, jumping off of Dylan's slow train during his born-again phase. The medieval \\"Our Prayer,\\" which opens the album, makes the \\"teenage symphony to God\\" concept a mite too explicit for my taste, although the segue into doowop, an abbreviated cover of the Crows' 1953 hit, \\"Gee,\\" and later loungemusic, demonstrates Wilson's ecumenical approach to vocal music. In his excellent Guardian review, Alexis Petridis hears \\"doowop, barbershop singing, ragtime, the mock-Polynesian Tiki culture that swept Hollywood in the 50s, pre-rock 'n' roll crooning, yodeling, cowboy movies, You Are My Sunshine.\\"  There is connective tissue like bass lines, the recurrent \\"Heroes and Villains\\" motif, and the return of \\"Our Prayer's\\" harmonies that bridge apparently disparate cuts, and give the album a greater cohesiveness and architecture, although in the end Smile still sounds like brilliant pastiche.  But \\"My Only Sunshine (The Old Master Painter/You Are My Sunshine)\\" and \\"I Wanna Be Around/Workshop\\" have a sort of ghostly melancholy that wasn't apparent on Wilson's 2004 version, since the coarsening of Wilson's voice gave it a quality of humorless earnestness at the loss of its youthful yearning and plaintive beauty. That melancholy ultimately shadows the Smile project much as songs like \\"In My Room\\" were the ache of sadness in the Beach Boys' oeuvre. It wasn't, after all, all smiles. \\"Isolated, dislocated, scared: Smile often sounds like the work of a very lonely man,\\" Petridis astutely observes.  Smile is an aural movie of the mind, particularly the cut to the cantina scene midway through \\"Heroes and Villains,\\" a capsule history of the West in 4:54, a cowboy flick, a South of the Border comedy sketch. It doesn't sound remotely like anything else by the Beach Boys.  According to Petridis, Jimi Hendrix dismissed the re-recorded version as \\"a psychedelic barbershop quartet.\\" On this I have to dissent with Jimi. Yet Petridis identifies a problem with the Beach Boys that I tried to address in earlier posts: \\"Hendrix clearly thought the Beach Boys sounded hopelessly square, and he too had a point. Wilson may have been a genius, the greatest songwriter and studio technician of the 60s, but he was never cool . . . [On LSD] what came gushing out was a warped version of straight-arrow,white-collar American pop culture, the stuff most rock stars in 1967 deemed hopelessly naff.\\" I believe that's correct, though much of that era's psychedelic experimentation hasn't worn well either.  Since for Wilson the Beatles were the gold standard, I've got to give the nod to the Fab Four not only as songwriters but as musicians. The version of Smile on disk one doesn't surpass much less equal the trio of albums that include Rubber Soul, Revolver and Sergeant Peppers. As early as \\"She Loves You,\\" the Beatles as a band were a consummate pleasure machine. The unit seemed to operate on a quasi-mystical plane of intuition and communication that few of us can only aspire to understand. They were incredibly tight.  This was vocally true of the Beach Boys, but in the end, Wilson had to rely on session musicians, and he lacked a collaborator commensurate to George Martin. Sometimes Wilson vacillates between a cappella singing and orchestral busyness. As a lyricist, Van Dyke Parks wasn't Lennon-McCartney by a long shot. His Joycean stream-of-consciousness approach tended to be hit and miss.  In addition to his drug use, record company pressures, and his own nagging perfectionism, Wilson faced resistance within the group, principally from Mike Love who charged that Wilson was \\"f______ with the formula\\" that made the Beach Boys commercially successful in the first place.  In the end what Wilson and Parks created was a work that evoked a childlike, even prepubescent innocence on the cusp of adulthood--\\"Wonderful,\\" the Beach Boys' version of \\"She's Leaving Home,\\" \\"Look (Song for Children)\\" and \\"Child Is Father Of The Man\\" all sound that theme--as well as a tour through and reversion to Americana, the gifts of daily life, the comedy of pratfalls, historical invocations of Plymouth rock `n' roll, home on the range, the \\"church of the American Indian,\\" and a rather dubious pun on the Grand Coulee Dam and the Chinese \\"coolie working the railroad.\\"  \\"Fire: The Elements\\" comes closest to the vertiginous closing chord of \\"A Day in the Life\\"; one feels that it alludes not only to the apocalyptic nature of Los Angeles weather but the chaos of war, assassination and riot raging right outside of Brian Wilson's sandbox circa 1967. In that context, Mike Love's \\"blossom world\\" in \\"Good Vibrations,\\" the end song, seems nothing more than a naive dream.  Rolling Stone's David Fricke concludes his review of the complete \\"five-CD ocean of music\\" by saying that the \\"greatest pop album ever made is still in here, somewhere,\\" but that begs the question of what is there. Reconstructed on the two-CD set, Smile falls short of the greatest work by Bob Dylan or the Beatles--it's less deep--for it relies on that oldest of American myths: our myth of innocence. It's also as beautiful and imperishable as that myth. But the project is tarnished by our knowledge that the surviving Beach Boys sued Brian Wilson after his Smile assemblage, prompting the release of these sessions.  Bad vibrations.  While Paul McCartney longed to \\"get back homeward\\" on the eve of the Beatles' breakup in 1970, perhaps what's truer is Thomas Wolfe's adage, You Can't Go Home Again. Childhood is a country from which we're permanently exiled. It's a paradise lost.  (Originally posted on jazzadvance.com)
0142000655|5.0|John Steinbeck uses the story of Cain of Abel to tell us a story about two families, how their paths cross and everyone they meet along the way.  There something amazing about this book. Every time I reread it, I read it slower and read more parts aloud and let the story decide the pace of its telling. I've most recenty listened to the audiobook, while not perfect, having someone speak aloud at a controlled pace has allowed me to picture each detail more completely. And the more detail I could hold in together, the richer the experience becomes.  The number of and the amount of time Steinbeck spends on non-central characters was something I used to find that tiresome at first, having to get to know a new character just when I stopped thinking he was going to put in another one. It was a long time before I reread it, but after that, it became easier to see that Steinbeck wanted to use these characters to more clearly lay down the chain of consequences. I have a feeling that Steinbeck wanted Salinas to be as much a character in the book as the Trasks and the Hamiltons and how, through its citizens coped with the changes from the post civil war era to the early 20th century. Steinbeck grew up in Salinas after all and parts of the story have to do with tracing his lineage and his childhood, and perhaps he wanted the readers to know the town as well as he did (and I by now think it's a perfectly reasonable, respectable decision).   Whether or not you dislike the antagonist of the book depends on what you're willing to accept but John does make an explanation for why she does what she does. This can make it harder what to think about her as a person, if at all. The ideas I had about all the characters at the start of the story weren't exactly the same in the end. In fact, by the end, all I could do was just take the info in and suspend my judgement until I don't know when. Perhaps that's the whole point. He writes as if this has happened to someone we know and lost contact with, someone we wouldn't judge because we know them personally. I guess that's how I know that Steinbeck accomplished his mission.

1451627289|5.0|I'm a fast reader, but I romped through this book.  It's an adventure story, a love story, as well as a thriller.  Did Oswald act alone? Imagine an America without the internet, fast food chains, cell phones, computers, color tv, cable, credit cards, i-pods or mcmansions. Then imagine an America with big cars, big families, respect for teachers, girls spending hours jump roping, community dances, and moms who stayed home, cooked real food and hung the family laundry outside to dry.  This is the world King remembers.  This was my childhood. I'd forgotten many of the details of American life in the late 50's/early 60s until King reminded me.  He made me want to step into that rabbit hole and go back to that time when my family didn't have a key to the front door and my dad left his car keys in the ignition.

There is powerful science woven into this tale, but with it comes the characters and plot of a children's movie matinee. In many ways this story is an extrapolation of the idea behind Fantastic Voyage, with a group of students being shrunk to millimeter sizing and forced to deal with nightmare predators. I was expecting something more along the lines of Swarm, focusing on actual nanotechnology rather than Honey I Shrunk the Kids. 1586487981|5.0|I can't say enough good things about this book.  The authors start from the premise that the very words \\"world poverty\\" are so overwhelming and intractable to laymen that one's first impulse is to retreat from the whole subject and pass this book by; however generous our instincts on first thought, \\"there is really no point\\", the two brilliant professors begin, since our very next thought will likely be: \\"our contribution would be a drop in the bucket, and the bucket probably leaks\\". They instantly captured my heart and mind with the next sentence: \\"This book is an invitation to think again, again: to turn away from the feeling that the fight against poverty is too overwhelming, and to start to think of the challenge as a set of concrete problems that, once properly identified and understood, can be solved one at a time.\\"  This is the surprisingly, wonderfully simple premise of the book-- rather than getting hung up on the \\"big questions\\" of what causes poverty and whether free markets and democracy are good for the poor, put these endless debates aside and allow the authors to focus us on \\"what really matters: not so much where the money comes from, but where it goes. This is a matter of choosing the right kind of project to fund -- food for the indigent, pensions for the elderly, or clinics for the ailing? -- and then figuring out how best to run it.\\"  In other words, beware analysis paralysis and drill all the way down to practical real-life problem-solving at the most concrete level.  Example: malaria continues to kill nearly one million African children a year, a number which can be cut in half or more by getting the children to sleep under insecticide-treated bed nets, if only they would.  Now here's the rub: unless you get right to the micro man-in-the-street level, you cannot know whether it is better to give the nets away freely or to charge a little bit for them or even to pay parents to assure their kids use them.  The authors illustrate that these need be seen not as abstract philosophical questions, but rather as concrete questions answered by studying individual behavior to test  whether a \\"freebie\\" devalues the bed-net such that it gets converted to a fishing net-- a  result that mmight differ if families were made to pay at least some of the cost or were actually paid to compel use of the nets.  Over and over, the authors hone in on fundamental behavior studies to show how much progress can be made in improving diets, healthcare, schooling, etc when good hard thinking produces the right ground-level questions to ask. Everyone should buy this book since it is written precisely for the every man in exquisitely accessible, nearly jargon-free prose. If you choose not to buy it, at the least spend a little time on the book's hugely helpful, beautifully illustrated [...] website. You will be inspired to follow many suggestions there for how to help and become confident that real progress is being made in the struggle to remediate world poverty.

The on/off button gives a bit more bass in video gaming sound, but not much.  The X41s don't feel as comfortable as the X11s do. I could walk about 14' away from the receiver until the sound started to crackle and lose reception.<br />The batteries go fast in the headpiece.....two AAA batteries didn't go very far, about 8hrs of play. I thought there was something wrong with the headset until i realized that the batteries were dead. The only thing lacking is the bass which is a shame for games like Battlefield 3, where I wanted to hear the \\"boom\\" of the gunfire and tanks roaring nearby....didn't really get that from these headphones.  I love the fact that they are wireless but I don't think they fit as comfortable as the X11s and the batteries in them die way too fast.  Plan on having a couple of extra sets available when playing for long durations.<br /><br />I returned these X41s to Best Buy and bought the Turtle Beach DX11...which is basically a pair of wired X11s along with a DSS (digital surround sound) emitter, which is the same emitter used in the wireless base of the X41s.....this little doodad is what makes the \\"surround sound\\" possible in a pair of headphones. 0670022950|4.0|Pinker provides a cautiously optimistic view of the dramatic reduction in violence over the past few centuries. He has tied together a wide variety of violent behavior (from genocide to cruelty to animals) into one broad pattern of people becoming more civilized.<br /><br />He mostly covers the west, and I wished for more about the trends in the mideast and the more peaceful parts of asia. And why does he avoid commenting on the reported homicide rate for Somalia that's less than the US - does he have reject the data as inaccurate, or does he want to ignore evidence that's inconsistent with his claim that the rise of leviathan reduced crime?<br /><br />The substance of the book is less reassuring than a casual glance would suggest. In particular, the section on the power law distribution of war deaths shows that the model he uses says the expected number of war deaths is infinite, apparently due to small probabilities of really destructive wars. It's easy to overlook this disturbing implication if you aren't reading carefully. I'm disappointed that Pinker didn't talk about this more - how is it altered if we try to incorporate the finiteness of the available population? Should we focus a lot of our attention on avoiding unlikely megawars?<br /><br />He cautiously speculates about possible causes (increased global communications, trade, democracy, self-control, reason, feminization, education, and intelligence via the Flynn effect).<br /><br />If you're an ideologue looking for an excuse to be offended, you're fairly sure to find one in this book.<br /><br />Pinker exaggerates the evidence for violence among hunter-gatherers (see the book Sex at Dawn for the other side of this debate). And the evidence Pinker does present is quite consistent with the hypothesis that violence increased when people switched from hunter-gatherers to stationary farmers.<br /><br />The book is very long, and that's not due to a simple desire to be thorough. Much of the evidence seems selected more for vividness and memorability (e.g. his evidence from fairy tales - not completely frivolous, but insignificant enough evidence that he wouldn't have put it in a peer-reviewed article).<br /><br />I didn't learn enough from it to justify the time spent, but the ideas in it deserve to be known more widely.from fairy tales - not completely frivolous, but insignificant enough evidence that he wouldn't have put it in a peer-reviewed article).  I didn't learn enough from it to justify the time spent, but the ideas in it deserve to be known more widely.

The captain decides to keep her contract himself and takes her to his plantation.<br /><br />There Lavinia, who is white, is sent to the kitchen house to live among the slaves and learn their trades.  This is fairly scandalous, but the Captain's family is not usual.  Belle, who runs the kitchen house is the Captain's daughter from a liaison with a slave woman.  The Captain's wife spends her days drugged up on laudanum, a form of morphine.  She has been driven mad by the loss of her babies, a common occurrence in this time period.<br /><br />Lavinia learns to love the slaves she grows up among.  They are the only family she knows and she calls the mother and father of the house slaves Mama and Papa the same as her playmates do.  When Lavinia is twelve, the mistress' sister comes on a family visit when the Captain dies.  She is shocked at Lavinia's situation and when the family decides to take the mistress back with them to Williamsburg, they also take Lavinia.  There she grows into a woman, beautiful and determined to return somehow to the plantation and the only family she has ever known.<br /><br />Grissom has written a story of slavery from an interesting angle, that of an indentured white servant who grows up with a slave family.  It allows her to show the plight of the slaves from a different viewpoint, along with the strong sense of family they hold, and the mechanisms they create in order to survive and thrive.  This book is recommended for readers of historical fiction.

0446573019|5.0|Best Baldacci novel to date.  David Baldacci's Zero Day is, in my opinion, his best mystery thriller yet--5+ stars.  Great plot, well written, crisp dialog, and a terrific new character in Army Criminal Investigation Command (CID) Warrant Officer John Puller--A true individual army of One.  I noticed quite a few reviewers have slammed Mr. Baldacci for stealing Lee Child's mega character Jack Reacher and repackaging him as John Puller.  I strongly disagree!  Jack Reacher is one of my favorite literary characters, and in John Puller Mr. Baldacci has done a terrific job of creating Reacher's literary doppelganger.  I do not view this as literary plagiarism, but rather a selfish opportunity to read twice as many great stories concerning the Reacher/Puller mystique:  How can anyone logically argue with that--in the end is it not all about the story?!  I sincerely hope Mr. Baldacci will write more novels with the John Puller character as this will give ME many MORE hours of enjoyable reading.  Yep, John Puller is similar to several other detective action hero types:  The aforementioned Jack Reacher and of course Paul Brenner of Nelson DeMille literary fame.  In Baldacci's Zero Day we are introduced to John Puller, a highly decorated combat Ranger and tough as nails Army CID Investigator.  He is considered by the Army to be their best criminal investigator.  He is assigned SOLO to investigate the horrific death of a DIA colonel and his family--A most unusual investigative army protocol.  But then this is a most unusual case as Puller soon discovers.  Working with the local female police investigating sergeant, Samantha Cole, soon more deaths occur as the case becomes more and more strange.  Nothing is what it appears on the surface and makes no logical sense.  Puller uses all his investigative wiles and soon he and Sam Cole are on the hunt for the brutal killers.  Terrifically written detective mystery.  Baldacci uses short crisp sentences to drive both the inductive and deductive investigative reasoning used by Warrant Officer Puller.  Like all great detective mysteries, Puller's flawless logic engages the reader as the mystery deepens and unfolds.  Once Baldacci hooks the reader in, which he does very early in the novel, you simply cannot put this book down.  Relatively short chapters help to further quickly develop the story.  Character development was superb.  John Puller is a very complicated  man.  Mr. Baldacci lays enough biographical groundwork concerning Puller's complex past that the reader will quickly grow to appreciate just who John Puller is.  Other characters were also developed as the story warranted.  All in all Mr. Baldacci packed a lot of character development into 435 pages which gave the story solid literary depth.  No gratuitous sex, language, or violence:  A Baldacci trademark.  Like Lee Child's Jack Reacher novels, most of Zero Day is a thinking investigative mystery novel interspersed with moments of quick, decisive action.  Puller is a man of great intellect and few words,  But when action is required he is quick to end any physical confrontation on his terms.  I liked this book immensely.  I had almost given up on Mr. Baldacci but this novel shows just what he can really do, and that is write an excellent detective mystery.  Yes I know some do not like the Puller/Reacher similarity, but to the selfish me it just means more good mysteries.  I hope Mr. Baldacci makes the John Puller character into a series as he has a real winner here.  What do you say Mr. Baldacci?  Looking forward to the next John Puller novel??

Christine is not hung over though and soon learns that she's no longer in twenties either but from the same decade as the middle aged man washing up in the bathroom. B005J4TLN4|4.0|Months later, my first vivid memory when someone mentions ATTACK THE BLOCK is of how it generated more good buzz than COWBOYS & ALIENS, another sci-fi feature which shared the same box office debut date, stateside (July 29, 2011). Except that COWBOYS & ALIENS lacked what ATTACK THE BLOCK brought to the table: a certain vitality, a sense of suspense, and an exuberant young cast that's years away from being jaded. So, yeah, if you're expecting loads of Nick Frost in this picture, be aware now that he's in only for several cameo appearances, what amounts to pretty much a supporting role. And, word to the wise, unlike previous films featuring Nick Frost, this one isn't a spoofy film. Or not blatantly anyway. Director Joe Cornish opts for a more straight-up sci-fi action thriller. With good bits of comedy. But characters fall victim to alien predations and there are moments of icky blood-letting and gore. Which to me are strong selling points.<br /><br />ATTACK THE BLOCK pits a crew of teenaged hoods against a localized alien invasion. The film's skimpy budget couldn't at all afford epic. In South London, on Guy Fawkes Night, as lights and sparklers festoon the atmosphere, Moses and and his street gang go about the business of mischief. A nurse named Sam (Jodie Whittaker), who lives in the neighborhood, is their latest victim, to the tune of a mugging. But then a meteorite hurtles down to earth, smashing onto a nearby automobile and so Sam, she makes her getaway.<br /><br />I guess you can blame the ringleader Moses (John Boyega, memorable). Moses is stoic and intense and not afraid to take matters into his own hands. He ends up offing an extraterrestrial - because in sci-fi films, chances are good that meteorites are actually vehicles for space aliens. Moses's action ushers in the plummeting of more meteorites, and true to sci-fi trope, more alien creatures emerge, this time a pack of jet black, furry monsters. These predatory things, exhibiting cruel bioluminescent fangs and an aura of sheer menace, evince a marked lacked of intelligence, as demonstrated by one creature's sheer inability to open a friggin' dumpster. It's actually rather sweet that our \\"heroes,\\" witnessing these meteorites descending onto their South London housing projects, get all excited about killing off more aliens.<br /><br />Director Joe Cornish purposely introduces these disenfranchised inner city youths to look as threatening as possible. The kids are fully hooded up and masked, silhouetted in shadow, ominous. Every suburban yuppie's nightmare. As the story progresses, we see the kids remove their layers, and we get a peek into their personalities and individual ticks. It's nothing new, but Cornish frames these characters really well and the kid actors are lively and likable, once the masks come off. And when the chips are down, they don't hesitate to stand together, to protect their block. And they go about this armed with baseball bats and samurai swords and near-cryptic South London slang. Doesn't anyone just say \\"Crikey!\\" anymore?<br /><br />COWBOYS & ALIENS, with its mega-budget, doesn't come close to being as watchable as ATTACK THE BLOCK which goes to show that, sometimes, you really hone in on what's important when limited to meager resources. The film makes do with generous application of practical effects and by opting for a big bad alien that's conducive to cutting cost. And, yeah, the film could've been funnier. It could've been scarier. But this film didn't intend to be another SHAUN OF THE DEAD. It's its own entity and comes with its own sense of dynamism. Cornish infuses the screen with smarts and heart and lots of energy, abetted by cinematographer Thomas Townend's vivid imagery and Basement Jaxx's throbbing score. And the kids, again, lend their own vibrancy and great enthusiasm; their interactions are highlight scenes for me. This is some refreshing take on ye olde space alien invasion, with attitude to burn and urban jungle ethics and braggadocio on full exhibit. Er, not that I condone folks getting mugged or pants being worn so low that your crack shows.<br /><br />The DVD's bonus stuff:<br /><br />- Audio Commentary with writer/director Joe Cornish with kid actors John Boyega, Alex Esmail, Franz Drameh, Simon Howard and Leeon Jones<br />- Audio Commentary with Joe Cornish and grown-up actors Jodie Whittaker, Luke Treadaway, and Nick Frost<br />- Audio Commentary with Joe Cornish and executive producer Edgar Wright<br />- \\"BEHIND THE BLOCK Behind the Scenes Documentary\\" (01:01:00 minutes long)<br />- \\"Creature Feature: Special Effects\\" - spotlight on creating the alien invaders (00:19:36 minutes)<br />- \\"Meet the Gang\\" - the kid actors introduce their characters (00:03:56)<br />- \\"Unfilmed Action - Unfilmed Sequences from the Movie\\" - Joe Cornish and teen actor Alex Esmail talk about scenes that were omitted; this feature partly with storyboards (00:04:45)<br />- \\"That's a Rap\\" - Fun with the kids on set as they try to rap (00:02:15)<br />- the UK trailer and the US Red-Band trailerraggadocio on full exhibit. 1597801585|5.0|This is the best science fiction novel that I have read for many years.  It is bleak and depressing, but that is a fair take on how the world is evolving.  It more than matches William Gibson in painting a future world.  The painting is richer, more unrelenting and more vivid.  It stays in one place, without travelling around, and it thus grinds the reader's face further into the dirt with each chapter, until he is suffocating but fascinated.  It has great contemporary relevance, in showing how genetic engineering could destroy civilization.  Never mind how likely this is, it could happen and it makes your flesh crawl.  It is not perfect.  The characters are not all fully developed, and there are one or two redundant plot twists.  The technology context is incomplete - it would spoil the story to say too much but while reading it think about solar energy.  But this is science fiction not futurology.   One of the great contemporary science fiction novels. I await the next one from this author.

0307593312|4.0|I found 1Q84 nowhere near as captivating as Murakami's best, but I did enjoy it. It struck me as a cross between a storybook and a piece of ambient music - a simple tale with an emotional picture painted via repetition.  Where I found other Murakami novels pulling me down the rabbithole by the throat until I'd turned the last page, 1Q84 pulled me in but then allowed me to comfortably assess the dimensions of my surroundings. The world painted is surreal, but hardly ambiguous. Some may not like this, and while I didn't hate it, that by-the-throat experience is why I love Murakami.  On the other hand, I think it's this straightforwardness that would make this book the easiest Murakami to adapt into a film. A lot of the most striking imagery is visual, not psychological. If Takashi Miike took the reins it could be cool as hell!  The best passages of the book fly by all too quickly (my favorite being in book 2), buffered between long stretches of exposition, but perhaps that was the point. Some readers may be into it, but if you've never read Murakami, start with The Wind-Up Bird Chronicle, Norwegian Wood, or if you have a taste for moderate sci-fi, Hard Boiled Wonderland and The End of the World.
1451648537|5.0|This book is a memory walk for me. I remember my first computer - a KIM, a bare circuit board with a small keyboard and machine language to make it run - and the Apple II I bought as soon as they entered the market. It was the time of possibilities. Steve Jobs is, of course, one of the two main forces behind personal computing (Gates is the other)and understanding Jobs is part of understanding the Apple of today. No doubt the products are legendary. Getting under the skin of the man, which this book manages to do (only because Jobs allowed it to be done), lets us see the almost-angry personality, driven to succeed. No doubt he got the very best out of his people. And yet there is a sadness to it. The bosses I've had, who embodied this same trait, are not the ones I befriended. 1439102813|4.0|\\"Still Alice\\" chronicles through fiction the memory erasure caused by that still incurable disease, Alzheimer's. While not stellar on literary prose standards, author Lisa Genova does a masterful portrayal of what the downward mental descent looks like from the perspective of the Alzheimers patient.   Especially poignant is that the gradual mental erasure of memories and degradation of intellectual strength happens to an accomplished college professor  author of groundbreaking books and a popular lecturer in the classroom and the lecture  circuit.  In essence, who is Alice when her identity as a thought leader evaporates? The title gives a hopeful answer, but I have to admit that as I watch loved ones around me succumb to Parkinson's, Huntington's, and Alzheimer's, I realize I am not yet ready to accept this. Reading \\"Still Alice\\" has been helpful in helping me wrestle with getting to a place of acceptance but I've still got a ways to go.

Eleven-year-old Jaycee Dugard was walking to her school bus stop in suburban South Lake Tahoe, California one morning when a car pulled over, the back door opened, and a stun gun triggered. Jaycee was abducted by a convicted sex offender and his wife and held in captivity for 18 years during which she gave birth to two daughters.     Jaycee's story through the news media has been well recorded ([...]) but this memoir recounts it from Jaycee's first person perspective. It is a difficult read emotionally not only because of the horrific facts, but also because one has to accept Jaycee's recounting of the experience on her terms. It's easy to make all kinds of assumptions about what the experience would have been like but Jaycee's unguarded voice and non-bitter tone didn't match what I had expected.     It's remarkable how as an eleven-year-old Jaycee found the psychological strength to manage the horror and to even make a life with her two daughters that did not descend into hatred turned toward others or herself. In the book she also recounts some of her abduction trauma counseling process and it's clear that survival is one thing, recovering from the violations and the lost years is another.

At the bequest of the dying patriarch, the investigation focuses on the 1968 disappearance and presumed murder of Harriet Vanger.<br /><br />The story is framed by vendetta's Blomberg against another Swedish industrial group, whose founder has its own rivalry with the Vangers. So romantic, and so much fun to watch!<br /><br />Anyways, Dr. Quinn is a pretty good series, one you won't be embarrassed to watch in front of your great aunt Bessie or your pre-teen children. The DVD packaging is perfect, in my opinion, and I like that the titles are easily visible on each DVD. B000002OOY|5.0|Jurassic Park is still a publishing phenomenon and a blockbuster film franchisetwenty years, 2 novels, four films, and countless companion and tie-in books later. Arguably the worlds most renowned film composer, John Williams, gives us music glorifying the dinosaurs, the whole prehistoric era, as well as the message: Dont mess with Mother Nature.<br /><br />Noted science fact and fiction novelist Michael Crichton, who won fame with his book, The Andromeda Strainabout a plague in Arizona and the desperate race to contain itwanted to experiment with a story about a boy and a pterodactyl (extinct flying reptile). Frustrated with early drafts, he finally landed on one of the greatest ideas in all of literature: a modern day theme park full of genetically cloned prehistoric creatureswhich the people world can visit for a hefty price. The most ambitious entertainment venture of its time  becomes the greatest race for survival of all time. When a team of scientists arrive to the park for inspection, things go terribly wrong.<br /><br />Thus one of the worlds greatest cinematic franchises was born.<br /><br />Jurassic Park is one of the first movies I ever remember seeing. Only The Land Before Time preceded it. My first exposure to the concept of a dinosaur. Godzilla, King Kong, The Hobbit plus Lord of the Rings, and aliens galore in every mutated monster movie, remain a fictionthey only exist in imagination. But dinosaurs were real. And a few may have survived  Im reminded that no matter how wonderful or corny you think they are, theyre absolute fact. As real as the history behind Braveheart, Ben-Hur, or The Passion of the Christ.<br /><br />There are three main themes on the CD :<br /><br />The four note rising raptor motif heard in tracks 5, 11, 7, 15, and 16 (which is a fragmented repeat of track 7). These unsettling notes are heard in French horn.<br /><br />The beautiful main theme for the park itself heard in tracks 2, 4, 7, 10, 13, and 16. Involving choir, brassy trumpets, and cello strings, it perfectly captures the mystery and wonder of the idea of Jurassic Park.<br /><br />The helicopter Journey to the Island motif heard in tracks 4, 7, and 16 is a trumpet blast of triumph with strings and clashing cymbals.<br /><br />Some other sections have what I call creepin around music in tracks 1, 3, 5, and 14. Tracks 3, 11, and 15 are bombastic adventure rides, action tracks with banging percussion, clanging bells, and cymbal crashes.<br /><br />While softer moments can be heard in tracks 10 and 12, the few breathers in a score that flows amazingly well, even out of film sequence.<br /><br />Another great aspect of the CD release is much of the score was featured in the film. Tracks 2,5, 11, 14, and 15 feature extended cue sections from the cutting room floor. If you listen carefully, you will appreciate an alternate Main Title crawl in track 2, more raptor danger in track 5, high wire panic for another minute in track 11, plus alternate finale music in track 14. Musical ideas that are better than the final edit!<br /><br />I remember hearing James Horners main titles to Land Before Time at age four. The string rose in harmony as the fish whipped across the screen. I was transported to a prehistoric world. I wanted to BE a dinosaur. Both CDs are similarly themed and constructed. Jurassic Park is the better one, and a welcome return to that mystery and wonder, presented in a different package. Instead of entering the Mesozoic from the POV of a creature, man is pitted against prehistoric beast in a modern setting. This is a boy's score through and through (I know personally one woman who liked it). It is a score that will make you jump with fear and floor you with its beauty and haunting power.<br /><br />It perfectly captures the mysteries of childhoodnamely initiation into discovering something bigger than yourselfand will be one you will listen to repeatedly and wear out over many happy years.<br /><br />Before Hollywood butchers a remake fifteen years from now, go out and get this wonderful piece of music before it sells out.<br /><br />NOTE: The CD Jurassic Park by John Williams has been rereleased for digital download as a COMPLETE AND UNCUT edition, featuring 11 minutes of previously unreleased music on 4 tracks of approximately 2 and 1/2 minutes each.
